INTRODUCTION 

The hook of hzekiel presents problems to both the 
scholar and the ordinary reader. i?or the scholar it 
raises questions of authorship, language, and dating; 
for the person who reads it as scripture, it seems a 
tangle of prophecies, visions, allegories, violent 
imagery, crudity, and even barbarism, hut a faithful 
and patient attempt to understand the book proves re¬ 
warding to any reader. 

It is not within the scope of this present work to 
deal with the problems of authorship and dating. Rather, 
an attempt has been made to understand the theology-^of 
the book as it stands , hven if the book is a compilation, 
it is important to realize the results of such a bringing 
together of ideas. The fact that there are critical 
problems in iiiZekiel is therefore not being pushed aside 
and ignored; these problems are not relevant to the ap¬ 
proach required for the present study. 

iJ'or a general introduction to the Book of Bzekiel, 
the relevant chapters in John Baterson’s The Ooodly fel ¬ 
lowship of the Brophets , and iiidgar J. uoodspeed’s The 
Story of the Bible , are recommended, although‘the latter 
is really too brief. 

since Chapters I to VI of this thesis are concerned 
with analysis, references have been included in the text

THE PROPOSITION

CHAPTER I 

THE PRCPOSITION 

All of Ezekiel’s prophecies concerning the sin and 
punishment of the children of Israel stem from one prop¬ 
osition which is set forth in 14: 12 - 23, Briefly, 
this proposition is stated in w. 12 - 14• 

The word of the Lord came to me, saying, 

”0 mortal man, if a land sin against me 
by acting treacherously, and I stretch out my 
hand against it, and break its staff of bread, 
and send famine upon it, and cut off from it 
man and beast, even though these three men 
were in the midst of it-“Noah, Daniel, and 
Job—they would by their righteousness save 
but themselves,” is the oracle of the Lord God, 

This may be restated simply as: If a land sin against 
Yhwh, he will punish it» 

The first question that arises is, to what land 
does this refer? Does the prophecy look beyond the 
Israelites, God’s Chosen People, to include other nations, 
or is it concerned solely with the inhabitants of Pal¬ 
estine? The Hebrew word for land here is y 1 V , and 
since it is used without the definite article the state¬ 
ment is general and does not refer to a particular land. 

It means ’’any land”. Also, the characters Hoah, Daniel, 
and Job are not solely Isrealitish: Daniel appears in 
Ugaritic mythology (Gordon, Ugaritic Mythology , ”Dan’il 
Epic”), Job was probably an Ddomite (Lindblom, La Compos ¬ 
ition du Livre de Job, pp. 7ff), and Noah’s story is

told in the pre-Israelite mythology of the Old Testament. 
The later specific condemnation or Jerusalem iv.21; shows, 
by contrast, that the word of xhwh here concerns all nat¬ 
ions, as it ultimately does in any case, oo it is to be 
understood that any land, any nation, can sin against ihvih. 
This, of course, immediately supports the doctrine that 
ihwh is the uod of all the earth, and also implies that 
It may be possible for the gentile nations to live in a 
relation to ihwh which does not involve sin that brings 
divine punishment. 

The proposition itself must be examined in more de¬ 
tail. now does a land sin against ihwh? The prophecy 
says "by acting treacherously", (trespassing grievously-- 
A.V.; committing a trespass-n. v.;. ihe nebrew wox*a 
which describes this act of sin is i»ytp meaning to act 
unfaithfully (Arabic cognate means "slander"), which is 
an important concept of sin for nzekiel. The prophecy 
does not say, however, against whom the treachery is com¬ 
mitted. of course it can be inferred from the context 
that it is ihwh who is betrayed, since if it were not so, 
the rest of the proposition would be invalid. Support 
for this may also be found in many of the other prophecies 
of denunciation, e.g. Jerusalem "has wickedly rebelled 
against my ordinances and statutes more than the nations 
and lands that are round about here." (5: 6). There is 
no doubt here that the nations are acting treacherously

against ihwh. 

The prophet had no naive ideas as to the raeaning of 
ihwh^s choice of Israel. Certainly he realized that 
th ;ugh ihv/h was demanding obedience from Israel, he was 
also demanding obedience from the other nations. This 
obedience involved, among other things, meeting moral 
standards which both Israel ana the gentiles recognized. 
This is important; it means that for ibzekiel values are 
not relative, as some ancients and moderns try to hold, 
but that there is a minimum basic morality which all 
nations recognize, in his booh The Abolition of Man , 

C. Lewis ably sets forth and defends the position of 
an objective system of values, tiis argument will not be 
summarized here, but a few sentences appropriate to the 
thought of Lzekiel follow*v. ”The truth finally becomes 
apparent that neither in any operation with factual prop¬ 
ositions nor in any appeal to instinct can the innovator 
find the basis for a system of values. Lone of the princ¬ 
iples he requires are to be found there: but they are 
to be found somewhere else..,.This thing which i have 
called for convenience the fao , and which others may call 
the Natural Law or Traditional Morality or the first 
Principles of Practical Reason or the first Platitudes, 
is not one among a series of possible systems of value. 

It is the sole source of all value judgments, if it is 
rejected, all value is rejected. If any value is retained,

it is retained... .V/liat purport to be new systems or 
(as they now call them) ’ideologies’, all consist of 
fragments from the Tap itself, arbitrarily wrenched from 
their context in the whole and then swollen to madness 
in their isolation, yet still owing to the Tap and to 
it alone such validity as they possess.” (pp. 31.33). 
Ezekiel, of course, does not argue in this abstract way, 
but he, like other prophets before him, assumes the exist¬ 
ence of this lap . It is through disobedience to, or dis¬ 
regard of, a universal morality that the unfaithfulness 
to Yhwh takes place: this is the sin which is to be 
punished. 

The proposition states that the punishment may come 
in four ways at least: famine, wild beasts, sword, pestil¬ 
ence. It is not necessary to think that these are the 
only ways that the sinful nation may be punished; it 
should be noted, though, that these four catastrophes 
show the type of punishment that may come. Yhv;h will 
not send militant hosts of heavenly beings such as later 
eschatologists envisioned, but he will work through earthly 
forces. It is not possible for the nation as a whole to 
escape chastisement. If there are righteous men in the 
nation—even Eoah, Daniel, and lob (w. 14, 16, 18, 20)-- 
they will save but themselves alone. 

The first half of the proposition has been consid¬ 
ered, and now the whole can be stated. ”If any land sins

was said above, this argument can be valid only if the 
basis, the primary standard, is the same for all nations. 
The school principal may strap the fourth-grade boys for 
fighting in the school-yard, but hov; much harder will he 
strap the eighth-graders for the same offence (I) for the 
simple reason that being older they should know better. 

So it is, according to the proposition, with the punish¬ 
ment meted out by Yhwh. Jerusalem, having received 
special ravela-tion from Yhwh, should know better than to 
sin against him. ^ How much more when i send against Jer¬ 
usalem my four deadly judgments--sword, famine, wild 
beasts, and pestilence--to cut off from it man and beast I^ 
(v. 21) 

Such, then, is the proposition which underlies the 
prophecies of Ezekiel. The next step will be to see how 
it is applied to Jerusalem. Before that, two things must 
be noted. First, that Ezekiel uses ^’Jerusalem^’ to refer 
both to the city and to the southern kingdom (possibly 
because the Temple was in that city and Ezekiel felt that 
as a result Jerusalem should be influencing the rest of 
the land for good). Second, no extensive attempt will 
be made to explain why the proposition appears so far on 
in the book of Ezekiel, and not at the beginning. The

CHAPTER II 

THE FORIvlAL CHARGE 

AGAINST JERUSALEM 

Up to this point the punishment of Jerusalem 
has rested upon an "if”-—the proposition set forth in 
14; 12 - 23. This is iramedlately followed by The 
Allegory of the ?/orthless Vine (15; 1 - 8) in which the 
prophet says the ”if« has become a reality, and Jerusalem 
is therefore charged with having sinned against Yhwh. 

”’I will make the land a desolation, because they have 
acted treacherously,’ is the oracle of the Lord God.” 

(15; 8) Here the verb is used again, and at this 

point a closer examination of the verbs used to describe 
the national sin should be made. 

There are three verbs in particular which Ezekiel 
uses to express the nations’ sin. On a proceeding page, 

^ « be unfaithful , has been dealt with. The second 

verb is nio be contentious , refractory , rebellious . 

”’Yet she has wickedly rebelled against my ordinances and 
statutes more than the nations and lands that are round 
about her...’” (5; 6) The Hiph’il imperfect is employed 
in this quotation, and might be translated as ’’insultingly 
rebelled”. The third verb is 1 •! D turn aside from . 

"...when I have broken their heart which hast apostatized from 
me...” (6:9) It is legitimate, though not necessary,

to translate this as apostatize; the significance is quite 
clear in the simple meaning of the verb. ^^But woe, indeed 
to them when 1 depart ( 1-I ^ )from them!** (Hosea 9: 12) 
Ezekiel uses these three verbs interchangeably, as has 
been shown, to express the sin of Jerusalem against Yhwh; 
it is not a matter of choice between the three: their mean 
ings are bound together, and might be compressed in the 
phrase: **In turning aside in insulting rebellion, Jerus¬ 
alem has betrayed her God.** This is the meaning of the 
formal charge against the nation, and Ezekiel elaborates 
upon it extensively. Jerusalera has sinned, and therefore 
will be punished. 

Following this statement of the charge in ch. 15, 
the prophet gives a detailed accoimt of the treachery. 

In the Allegory of the Faithless Wife, Yhwh summarizes 
the birth and history of Jerusalem including his covenant 
with her. For Ezekiel, the covenant relation is import¬ 
ant. The account is given in typically Hebrew (oriental) 
Imagery. At birth, Jerusalem was the uncared-for child 
of a mixed marriage--anc Amorite married to a HittitSo

were born your navel-string was not cut, nor 
were you washed with water to cleanse you, 
nor were you salted or wrapped in swaddling 
clothes--no eye had pity enough upon you to 
do any of these things out of compassion for 
you--but you were cast upon the open field, 
with no regard for your life, on the day that 
you were born. Then I passed by you, and saw

as you lay weltering in your blood, ’Live, 
and grow like a plant of the field.’ So 
you grew big and strong, till you reached 
the time of your maturity.” (16: 4 - 7a) 

Yhwh adopted this waif, entered into a covenant with 
her, and brought her up, giving her everything of the 
best, and she grew into a beautiful woman. (16: 8 - 14). 

The impression given by much of the Book of Ezekiel is of 
a harsh, stern, indignant God, but here is an excellent 
illustration of the graciousness of Yhwh. It is not 
right to say that the God of the Old Testament is a dif¬ 
ferent God from the God of the New Testament, that one 
is a stern judge and the other a God of love. The attrib¬ 
utes of God (his judgments, his graciousness) are aspects 
of the same thing. Those who understand his love under¬ 
stand his wrath. ”It is a fearful thing to fall into the 
hands of the living God’.” (Hebrews 10: 31). 

Jerusalem, having grown beautiful, turned away from 
Yhwh to other ”lovers”. (16: 15 - 34). That is, she 
forsook the covenant and purpose of Yhwh to establish her¬ 
self among the nations by dabbling in international 
affairs, with the consequent involvement in pagan religions. 

”But you trusted in your beauty, and 
played the harlot on your reputation; you 
lavished your harlotries on everyone who 
passed*by... And with all your abominable 
harlotries you did not remember your youth¬ 
ful days, when you were naked and bare, and 
lay weltering in your blood...You played the 
harlot with your gross neighbors, the Egypt¬ 
ians, multiplying your harlotries to vex me.

So I stretched out my hand against you, re¬ 
duced your allowance, and handed you over to 
the will of your enemies, the daughters of the 
Philistines, vi/ho were ashamed of your lewd 
conduct. But you played the harlot with the 
Assyrians, being still unsated; and when you 
had played the harlot with them you were still 
unsated. So you multiplied your harlotries 
with that land of traders, Chaldea, and even 
then you were unsated.’’ (16: 15,22,26-29) 

Egypt, Philistia, Assyria, and Chaldea became Jerusalem’s 

"lovers”. Why, the relationship was not even on the 

common immoral level of the harlot of the streets I The 

latter is solicited and paid by men, but Jerusalem, as 

a harlot, did the soliciting and the paying. (16:31 - 34). 

She is a nymphomaniac, consumed by an insatiable lust 

for which she is responsible, in forsaking ihwh she has 

forsaken that which alone could make her life truly good, 

and save her from her lust for power. She is therefore 

to be punished. 

In the acted parable of the Prophet’s Hair (5: Iff) 
the charge is made again.

of the nations, with lands round about her.

( emended text) than the nations that are

nor obeying my ordinances, nor even conforming 
to the practices of the nations that are round 
about you’—therefore thus says the Lord God:

execute judgements in the midst of you in the 
sight of the nations. Because of all your 
abominable deeds 1 will do among you what I 
have never done before, and the like of which 
1 will never do again.*’* (5: 5 - 9) 

Jerusalem was chosen by Yhwh but has rebelled and behaved 

in a manner worse than the **unenlightened" nations. In 

this passage there is a further insight into the meaning 

of rebellion, it consists in not following ichwh’s statutes 

( ilipn ), nor obeying his ordinances l^or 

even conforming to the practices of the nations round about. 

These are her abominable deeds. She has sunk below the min- 

imum requirements for any nation, and is to be punished. 

A further development of this theme is found in 
20: 1 - 44. Here reference is made to the first two com¬ 
mandments (Exodus 20: 1 - 5). Along with Deuteronomy 
6: 4ff (Shema) and Jesus (Matthew 22: 37), Ezekiel sees 
these as the most important, or basic conditions of Yhwh’s 
choice of Israel. 


1. from the very OOV to judge « govern ; 

the noun’ is best understood as'meaning the decision 
of a judge or person of authority. Once such a dec¬ 
ision was given, it usually became a law through pre¬ 
cedence. e.g. I Samuel 30: 24. But Yhwh gives the 
law-giver, hence all can be ascribed to him.

♦♦’Thus says the Lord God: On the day 
that I chose Israel, and swore by uplifted 
hand to the descendants of the house of Jacob, 
revealing myself to them in the land of Egypt, 
and swearing by uplifted hand to them, saying, 

”I am the Lord your God”—on that day I swore 
by uplifted hand to them, that I would bring 
them out of the land of Egypt to a land that 
I had given to them, a land flowing with milk 
and honey, a land which is the glory of all 
lands; and I said to them, ”Cast away each of 
you the detestable things which you love, and 
do not defile yourselves with the idols of 
since i am the Lord your Godo” But 
they rebelled against me and would not listen 
to me; they did not cast away each one the 
detestable things which they loved, nor did 
they forsake the idols of Egypt.*” (20: 5 - 8) 

And Yhwh punished Israel, but in doing so he had regard 
for his honor”...that it might not be profaned in the 
sight of the nations among whom they lived, and in whose 
sight I revealed myself to them...” (20: 9,14,22). 

Such is the nature of God, yet Israel did its worst to 
profane that honor, and is to be punished, 

Israel is a rebellious house, 'iJD Jig (12: 2). 

The list of malpractices is almost endless: Yhwh’s holy 
things are despised, his sabbaths profaned; slander, 
murder, eating flesh with the blood in it, lewdness, 
adultery, incest, extortion, oppression, robbery, in¬ 
justice; human sacrifice, profanation of the sanctuary, 
and on and on. (22: 8 - 12, 29 - 31j 23: 37 -45; 44: 6 - 8) 
”And me you forget I” (22: 12). Not only has the special 
covenant with Yhwh been broken, but the universal moral 
law, that which G. S. Lewis calls the Tap (supra pp, 4-5),

has been deliberately cast aside. It wasn’t that Israel 
had been given but one chance, she had had many, and she 
should have learned by the experience. Jerusalem (Judah, 
the southern kingdom) could have learned from the exper¬ 
ience of Israel (the northern kingdom). 

Bacon, Novum Organum , XCIV . quotes: "That which is 
worst with regard to the past should appear most consola¬ 
tory for the future; for if you had done all that your 
duty conmianded, and your affairs proceeded no better, 
you could not even hope for their improvement; but since 
their present unhappy situation is not owing to the force 
of circumstances, but to your own errors, you have reason 
to hope that by banishing or correcting the latter you 
can produce a great change for the better in the former.” 

An echo of the Allegory of the l“wo Sisters (23: 1 - 49). 
Samaria and Jerusalem are compared to two sisters who 
turned harlot. Both had been in Egypt when they played 
the harlot. Then Oholah (Samaria) played the harlot with 
Assyria, and Yhwh handed her over to her lovers for punish¬ 
ment (23: 5 - 10). Her sister, Oholibah, (Jerusalem) did 
not learn from her sister’s experience, but carried on 
even worse practices with Chaldea. "Therefore thus says 
the Lord God: ’Because you have forgotten me and cast 
me behind your back, you must bear the consequences of 
your lewd harlotries.’” (23: 35)

and in keeping with it, she is to he punished, "Because 
you did not remember your youthful days, but roused me 
to wrath with all these things, l on my part will requite 
your doings upon your head*" (16: 43) There is no need 
to examine other such passages, though two more may be 
noted: 16: 44 - 52; 4: 4; and other verses in the passages 
already referred to. 

As a result of this analysis, the meaning of the 
national sin has been shown* In primitive Israel (10th c* 
B*C.) there were two concepts about sin. First, it was 
a defilement which separated man from God. The nature 
of this defilement was not clearly defined, but it in¬ 
volved both the moral and the tabu. Second, when dis¬ 
aster occurred, men asked what caused it, i.e., sin brought 
disaster, and disaster was the result of sin. When Uzzah 
touched the ark (2 Samuel 6: 1 - 7) he died, evidently 
because he v/as not a priest, and therefore not entitled 
to touch the ark. At another time, Saul wanted an answer 
from the oracle, but got none because someone had sinned 
—Jonathan had broken Saul’s vow of fasting, and although 
this was done in complete ignorance it brought disaster 
(1 Samuel 14: 37 - 44). However these may be explained, 
it must be recognized that a distinction was being made 
between right and wrong. There were those things which 
"just aren’t done" in Israel (2 Samuel 13: 12), and to 
do them brought disaster.

writer looked at all the trials and troubles of Israel

through a vision of the holiness of God. xt is pride, 
primarily, that causes sin iIsaiah 2: 17 - 18)* 

Deuteronomy sums up the early prophets—the cardinal 
sin is pride, which causes men to forget the love of God, 
and is shown hy worldliness, materialism, and/or idol¬ 
atry. (Deuteronomy 8: 11 -14). ^Din is an offense 
against God, talcing the form of a breach of the laws 
God has set up for society. (There is no distinction 
between crime and sin). 

Jeremiah saw sin as unnatural, or as man disobey¬ 
ing nature (Jeremiah 2; 14; 8: 7), though it is difficult 
to say hov/ far he would carry this idea. His search for 
a just and honest man (Jeremiah 5: Iff.) resulted in fail¬ 
ure, showing the universality of sin, yet the prophet 
believedthat God could and would cure sin^—with the new 
covenant (Jeremiah 31: 31 - 34). This would be based on 
forgiveness: ”...for x will pardon their guilt, and their 
sin will 1 remember no more.’’ (Jeremiah 31: 34) The 
exile was to be punishment for sin. 

Ezekiel, then, stands in line with the general O.T. 
view of sin. Yhwh chose Israel not for any merit of her 
own, but out of his love, his graciousness (16: 6), and 
established a covenant with her. But Israel betrayed 
Yhwh by turning aside from him in insulting rebellion 
which arose out of her pride, trusting in herself (16: 15). 
This pride, as will presently be seen, is an attempt on

the part of man to play Gk)d—to know of himself what is 
right and wrong, good and evil (Genesis 3: 22), Man is 
divine in that he can make a distinction between right 
and v/rong, but when he makes this distinction apart from 
Yhwh he is trying to make himself God, and that is pride, 
the cardinal sin. This, says Ezekiel, is what Jerusalem 
has done: she has sinned against the gloiy of i:hwh»giv¬ 
ing him a bad name among the nations, and surely she will 
be punished, for there is no vicarious righteousness; 
the righteousness of the few will not save the many. In 
fact, the righteous will suffer with the wicked (21: 4).

THE PUNISHMENT OF JERUSALEM 





21 


CHAPTER III 

TliE PUNISHI^NT 

OP JERUSALEM 


”My object all sublime I shall achieve in time, 
To let the punishment fit the crime....” 

(W. S. Gilbert, 
The Mikado) . 


Because of her treacherous behavior, Jerusalem, the 
harlot, is to be judged and punished as such. 

"...behold, I am assembling all your 
lovers, whose embraces you have enjoyed-- 
all whom you loved, as well as all for 
whom you cared nothing—I will assemble 
them from every quarter, and will expose 
your nakedness for them to gaze at. I 
will judge you as women are judged who 
break wedlock and shed blood, and i will 
turn you over to my bloody fury and indig¬ 
nation; I will hand you over to your lovers, 
and they shall tear down your shrines and 
demolish your resorts...they shall bring an 
assemblage of people against you, who shall 
stone you and slash you with swords...so 
will I put a stop to your harlotry, and 
you shall no longer pay hire to your lovers 
....” (16; 37 - 41) 

It is the lovers who are to carry out the punish¬ 
ment. The places of Baal-worship will be destroyed, and 
all the vestiges of civilization will be ripped from 
Jerusalem, leaving her "naked and bare,” It is the great 
irony of the villain defeated by his own villainy. The 
Babylonians, Chaldeans, the men of Pekod, Shoa, and

Koa , and the Assyrians shall come against the sinful 
nation, (23: 23). These, again, are the lovers. 

"And they shall come against you with an 
army of chariots and wheels, and with a host of 
men on foot; they shall array themselves against 
you on every side with luckier, shield and 
helmet; 1 will entrust to them the execut¬ 
ion of judgment, and they shall execute their 
judgment upon you. 1 will stamp you with the 
seal of my indignation...." (23: 24) 

"As 1 took proceedings against your fathers in the 
desert of the land of iiigypt, so will I take proceed¬ 
ings against you" (20: 36). At least three of the four 
deadly judgments mentioned in the proposition are to 
come upon the land. 


Without is the sword, and within are pestil¬ 
ence and famine; he who is in the open field 
shall die by the sv/ord, and he who is in the 
city shall be devoured by famine and pestil¬ 
ence; and if any survivors escape, they shall 
be like doves of the valley upon the mountains, 
all of them moaning over their guilt. (7: 1§,16) 

Instead of wild beasts, other judgments will come: 


"Behold, I am kindling a fire in you, which 
shall devour every green tree and every dry 
tree in you; the flaming flame shall not be 
quenched, and every face from south'to north 
shall be scorched by it. And all flesh shall 
see that I the Lord have kindled it eo that 
it may not be quenched." (20: 47, 48) 


1. Pekod, Shoa, and Koa ( )are dif¬ 

ficult to identify. Pekod wa~s possibly a tribe in 
south-eastern Babylon, and forming part of the Baby¬ 
lonian army, Shoa is usually identified as a group 
of nomads inhabiting Mesapotamia and lands east of 
the Tigris. Koa, perhaps was a people from east of 
the Tigris, but there is very little evidence. See 
Brown, Driver, and Briggs* Lexicon. All that dan be 
said is that these people seem to have been elements 
in the Babylonian army.

"I will scatter you aiaong the nations and 
disperse you over the lands; 1 will consume 
your uncleanness out of you, and you shall be 
dishonored in the sight of the nations; so 
shall you know that 1 am the Lord.'^ (28: 15, 16) 

In the parable of the Prophet’s Hair (5: Iff*) the des¬ 
truction is.neatly divided into three sections; one-third 
of the people are to die of pestilence or famine, one- 
third are to fall by the ’’sword round about you”, and one- 
tnird are to be scattered to the winds, pursued by the 
sword. This punishment is to be a reproach, horror, and 
a warning to the nations round about, A time limit of 
forty years has been set for the punishment (4; 1 - 17)* 

The kinS of Babylon is to play the major role in the act, 
and is to be guided to his work by the prophet. The king 
may think that his actions are based upon divinations by 
arrows, teraphim, and liver, but such is not the case. 

Yhwh is bringing him against Jerusalem (21; 19 - 23). 

r 

This is reminiscent of Isaiah 10; 5 - 11; 

0 Assyria, rod of my anger, 

And staff of my furyI 

Against a godless nation I send him. 

And against the people of my wrath I 
charge him, .,. 

But not so does he think. 

And not so does he plan;.,, 
for he says, 

”Are not my captains all of them kings?... 

Shall 1 not do to Jerusalem and its images. 

As 1 have done to Samaria^ and its idols?” 

Whereas Isaiah saw Sennacherib as the one who would be 

brought by Yhwh to carry out his punishment upon the people,

iilzekiel sees Nebuchadnezzar as the instrument. The pro¬ 
phet Ezekiel even puts the destruction by sv^ovd into a 
song (21: 9ff.). It should be noted in passing that all 
good prophecy is set in a historical context, i.e, both 
Isaiah and Ezekiel knew that the great powers were on the 
war-path, and that Israel was an obstacle in the path. 

Both prophets maintained that if Israel had kept out of 
international affairs she would not have been that ob¬ 
stacle. "Thus will 1 put a stop to the lewdness in the 
land". (23: 48). 

But it is clear to the prophet that the major factor 
in the punishment is not the carnage, but the deportation, 
the exile in Babylon. In the acted parable. Symbols of 
Exile, (12: 1-20) this deportation is depicted. Al¬ 
though this is specially against "the prince who is in 
the midst of them" it shows in general that the people 
will be taken to Babylon and scattered to the four winds, 
followed by the sword. (This latter is a way of saying 
that the event will be the result of, and carried out by, 
military conquest). As is known, there were two such 
deportations. The first, 597 B.C., involved the removal 
of the upper classes and influential people. The second, 
in 586 B. C., saw the removal of most of the rest of the 
people. Ezekiel was taken with the first group, and a 
messenger brought him the nev7s of the second capture. 

(33: 21ff).

”0 mortal man, those who inhabit these 
ruins in the land of Israel keep on saying, 

»Abraham was but one man, yet he received 
possession of the land; now, we being many, 
the land will surely be given into our pos¬ 
session iherefore say to them, ’Thus says 
the Lord God: You eat flesh with the blood in 
it, you lift up your eyes to your iaols, you 
shed blood, and shall you have possession of 
the land? lou resort to the sword, you commit 
abominations, you defile one another’s wives, 
and shall you have possession or the land?’” 

(33: 24-26) 

These left-overs (c.f. 2 Kings 25: 22) seem to have been 
the scum of the population, those not worth taking into 
exile—they certainly acted the part. They claimed that 
the land was now theirs, but they forgot that Yhwh had 
his plans to fulfil, and had not brought forth the de¬ 
portation in order that such scum (who were continuing 
the malpractices) should inherit the earth. They are to 
be destroyed by lln.vjh that his cleansing of the city may 
be complete, (33: 27 - 29) 

An interesting description of the great catastrophe 
is found in the picture of the Day of Doom (7: 1 - 27), 
Here the relentless judgment of Ihwh v«?orks itself out 
upon the nation, ”An end has come”-the end has corae-- 
it awakes against you,,,.Behold, the dayS See, it 
come si Doom advances. Pride has blossomed, insolence 
has budded, violence has grown into a shoot of vv^icked- 
ness....” (7: 6, 10, 11) Again and again the refrain 
comes that Israel is to be punished according to her 
ways: ”for in accordance with their ways will I treat

them, and in accordance with their practices will I 
judge them....’^ (7: 27), 

At this point it would be well to examine the 
Allegory of the Rusty Pot (24: 1 - 14), because it, too, 
deals with the judgment. 

"Set on the pot, set it on, 

And pour water into it; 

Put into it the pieces. 

All the good pieces, the thigh 
and the shoulder; 

Fill it with the choicest bones, 

Taken from the choicest of the flock; 

Pile wood under it, let it boil well, 

That the bones also may be seethed in 
the midst of it." (24: 3b - 5) 

The bloody city is the pot, full of filth v^hich never 

leaves her (vv. 6 - 8). Yhwh himself is going to build 

a fire under this pot to cleanse it, but this in itself 

will not be enough, for it will leave the pot stained. 

So Yhwh will set the pot empty upon the embers and heat 

it to remove the stain. Bertholet and Galling ( Hesekiel ,^ y 

p. 87), state that in the prophecy the pot is melted, not 

just heated red-hot as the Smith-Goodspeed translation 

suggests. That the pot should melt fits in better with 

the rest of the prophecy, and with subsequent history. 

As Bertholet and Galling point out (op. cit., p. 85), 

this allegory is in two sections: vv. 1-5 set forth 

the bare fact of Jerusalem’s seige in a symbol, and 

vv. 6-14 give an elaboration of the meaning of this 

acted parable.

Assuining that Ezekiel is exilic, the action took 
place on the anniversary of the fall of Jerusalem, but 
if the passage is imiaediately pre-exilic, the assumption 
is not necessary. Nebudchadnezzar appeared in 588 B.C., 
and as the seige began, Ezekiel set up the pot for stew. 
The verb used in the second member of v. 2 is Tj p 
lean, rest, and this passage may be translated as: the 
king of Babylon hath leaned against ~ ) Jerusalem; 

in this sense it may be read: the king of BalDylon hath 
invested Jerusalem. This reading supports the idea that 
the whole passage is pre-exilic. 

The emphasis is upon the stain or rustiness of the 
pot, which stands for the impurity of the city, caused 
by blood-guiltiness, w. 7-8 are the explanation of 
the blood-guiltiness of Jerusalem, whose citizens shed 
blood shamelessly (cf. 23: 45)^—-they were worse than 
their neighbors. Blood is indestructible when left 
uncovered, and cries out for revenge (Genesis 4: 10). 
There is only one way, therefore, that the blood (stain, 
rust) can be got rid of--heat the kettle till it melts 
and runs dovvn into the fire. This means that Jerusalem 
will have to annihilated for cleansing. The emptying 
of the pot of the pieces is the exile, the melting is 
the destruction of the city. The allegory then contains 
these symbols: the pot is Jerusalem, the flesh is the 
people, the choice pieces are the leaders, the fire is

the seige; taking out the pieces is the exile of some 
of the citizens (the first exile); the blood shed is 
the child-sacrifice, general injustice, moral foulness, 
and disobedience; and the melting of the pot is Yhwh’s 
justice. 

It is commonly known that the exiles in Babylon 
were in reasonably favorable circumstances, ihere is 
little evidence for this in Ezekiel, v^zhich is not surp¬ 
rising, since he was concerned with the spiritual wel¬ 
fare of the people. What evidence there is linked with 
a prophecy dealing with the attitude of the exiles: 

”As for you, 0 mortal man, your fellow- 
countrymen v/ho talk of you by the walls and 
at the doors of the houses say to one another, 
’Comeand hear what the word is that comes 
from the Lordl* Ihey come to you, as my 
people used to come; and they sit before you, 
as if they were still my people: they listen 
to your words, but they will not obey them; 
for with their mouths they make a show of 
love, but their minds are set upon their own 
selfish gain. You are to them like a singer 
of love songs, with a beautiful voice, and 
able to plajr well on the instrument; they 
listen to your words, but they will not obey 
them. Only when the hour comes--and it is 
coming—they shall know that a prophet has 
been in the midst of them.” (33: 30 - 33) 


Such was the condition of the exiles. They made a show 
of being religious, but were really after the money they 
could make. Their show of religion may have been brought 
on by the news of the final fall of the city (33: 21ff) 
and the hope that, since Ezekiel’s prophecies about the 
fall of the city' have come true, perhaps his prophecies

here to indicate that although the exile was a national 
disaster,it was not an exile into slavery, it was a 
removal of the flesh from the pot so that the pot could 
be cleaned, a cleaning which meant the destruction of 
the pot. Ezekiel implied that the pot would be re-cast, 
but did not actually carry his analogy that far* 

The first great event in the history of Israel was 
the Exodus which brought the people national status, and 
this was removed by the exile, ^et Israel survived the 
exile, a unique event in religious history. This was 
largely due to the interpretations which three of the 
great prophets put upon that event. The three were 
Jeremiah, Ezekiel, and i^eutero-isaiah. There were, of 
course, other explanations, current even among the exiles, 
and some of these will be examined first. 

In Jeremiah 44: 16 - 18, the Jev^js who had fled to 
Egypt and Pathros say that the exile is the result of 
Josiah’s reform. Josiah had abolished many religious 
practices on the ground that they were pagan; evidently 
there was much opposition to him and it went underground 
until his death. It maintained that pagan practices had

kept the people happy and secure in Jerusalem; when

A second theory was that i:hwh had deserted Israel, 
in the vision of the Valley (of bones) the people cry 
^’Our bones are dried up, and our hope is lost; we are 
completely cut off.” (37; 11) Jlov/ever, a more accurate 
interpretation of this verse is that the bones are dried 
up because the Jews have lost hope, furthermore, sub¬ 
sequent events showed that they were not cut off from 
Yhv/h. 

A third theory is found in Jeremiah 15; 4 —^Judah 
is being punished for ivxanasseh’s sins. This is also used 
in 2 xhings; 25 - 26, and is a variation of the ^’sour 
grapes" theory which fzekiel refutes (18; 1 - 32). it 
is likely, however, that Jeremiah meant that Manasseh 
started practices which did not cease when he died, and 
therefore the people were being punished. This would 
come closer to Ezekiel^s interpretation. 

At first, Jeremiah supported Josiah^s reforms, but 
he soon saw that these did not go deep enough, since they 
dealt with the cult and not v;ith the moral life. Jer¬ 
emiah, holding iiosea’'s equation of sin with idolatry, 
finally came to prophesy the downfall of Jerusalem because 
only a divine act and a nev/ covenant could put the people 
in the right relation to Yhwh.

with iLzekiel’s theory of the exile. According to this, 
Israel’s sin was against the glory of ihwh, against his 
presence, character, and reputation, not just his honor. 
The sin destroys the holiness of the community, so ^hwh 
cannot dwell in it. 

Then the cherubim lifted their wings 
--the wheels remaining beside them, and the 
glory of the nord resting above them--and 
the glor^^ of the Lord rose from the midst 
of the city, ana stood upon the mountain east 
of the city. [11: 22 - £3) 

So Yhwh’s people go into exile as punishment. Kather 
than proving his defeat, this proves Yhwh’s high moral¬ 
ity: he would rather cast off his people than have his 
glory marred. ’’The world exists for the glorj^ of lahveh, 
and the goal of history is not reached till,iin Exekiel’s 
favourite v\rords, it is known and understood of Gentile 
as well as Jew, though in a very different sense and with 
very different results by one than by the other, that 
’I amYahveh.’” (Montefiore, op. cit,, loc. cit.) 

Now it could be said that the Jews and ge^ntiles 
understood that ’I am Yhwh’ after the exile took place. 
Would that not then be the end of the matter? The 
prophetic view was that Yhwh had a purpose which Israel 
was chosen to carry out, something Israel failed to 
do. If Yhwh is the great God that he claims to be, he 
cannot be defeated by the sins of a nation, so he must

uphold his righteousness by punishing his people. This 
is the dilemiaa: Yhwh must punish Israel or be disgraced. 
The solution of a dilenmia rests in finding a third pos¬ 
sible choice, and that is what Kzekiel did. ihwh’s 
purpose will be carried out by redeeming Israel, 

But doesn’t this make Yhwh too concerned for his 
reputation? Not if it is understood what is meant by 

Yhwh’s ’’name”. For all primitive peoples in general, 

1 

and the hebrews in particular, names were important. 

They were taken from a variety of sources, but were 
for the most part attempts to describe the character 
of the person being named, his reputation, or of some 
event with which he was connected. As a result names 
had to be changed if there were a change in character 
or occupation. The classic example is Jacob, ”A cheat”. 
After he wrestled with the angel at Feniel, his name 
was changed to Israel, ”A perseverer with God,” (Gen¬ 
esis 32: 22 - 28) These names show the nature and 
revealed character of the owner, and so it is with the 
names of God. There is a great variety of names for God 
in the Old Testament, and chief among these is”Yhwh? but 
the meaning, as well as the spelling of this name, is 
in dispute, in Exodus 3: 13 - 15, the discussion of 


1, Hastings, Encyclopaedia of Religion and Ethics , subject 
title ”Names” contains a good discussion of this matter

this name shows that it is obviously connected with

T f - ■ 

or to happen ), but just which part of the verb it is can¬ 
not now be said. There is a choice between he will be 
and he will cause to be . (the Authorized Version minstrans- 
lates it as present tense, I am that I am ; Dr. Moffat’s 
The Eternal is not much better). Granted that the origin¬ 
al meaning is obscure, from Exodus 3 on there is a definite 
connection with n' H , for the passage is a deliberate 

attempt to show that God’s character is symbolized or 
described by the word "Thwh". In this Exodus incident, 
the idea is either that God will be with Moses in Egypt 
as well as in the desert, or else that he is guarantee- 
in that what he promises will come to pass. An excel¬ 
lent discussion of this is given in Buber’s Moses , p. 39ff« 
Enough has been said here, however, to show that the name 
is an attempt to tell something of God’s character. It 
is in this sense that Deuteronomy says ’’for I proclaim 
the name of the Lord” (32: 3) and the context of this 
clearly shows the reference is to God’s revealed nature. 

What is this nature? The answer to this question 
is attempted in many ways and includes the attributes of 
IHiwh (holiness, righteousness, loyal-love, etc.), and the 
interpretation of history. The latter will be discussed 
in the next chapter, and it is sufficient here to say that 
it involves Yhwh’s purpose. Thus there is a connection

between Yhwh’s nature and his purpose, and when he does 
sometning for his name’s sake, he does it in accord with 
this nature and purpose, it was this idea Ezekiel used 
to interpret the exile, as will be seen; and this is what 
he meant by the continual use of the refrain : ”so shall

evident that these cannot be completely separated. 

The punishment, then, may be carried out in indig¬ 
nation and fury, but it is not carried out in a rage of 
ill tempter. It has a purpose. This had been slightly 
mentioned in the above discussion, and will be dealt with 
in detail in the next chapter: the Pardon and Kestoration.

PARDON AND RESTORATION 




36- 


CHAPTER IV 

PARDON AND RESTORATION 


The condition of the exiles as mentioned in 
33: 30 - 33, has already been dealt with, A further 
insight is given in the vision of the Valley (of bones), 
37; 1 -14. The prophet is carried out by the spirit and 
set down in a valley full of bones. The "bones are the 
whole house of Israel". Then Ezekiel is told by ^hwh 
to prophesy over the bones to the effect that they will 
be covered with sinews and flesh and receive breath 
( nil ) and live. This the prophet does, and the 

bones come to life. Despite the popular Negro Spiritual, 
it is ithwh and not Ezekiel who connects "dem dry bones". 
The vision is then explained for the prophet. 

"0 mortal man, these bones are the whole 
house of Israel. Behold, tney keep syaing, 

’Our bones are dried up, and our hope is 
lost; we are completely cut off.’ Therefore 
prophesy, and say to them, ’Thus says the 
Lord God: Behold, I am opening your graves, 

0 my people, and will bring you into the land 
of Israel. And when i open your graves, and 
raise you out of your graves, 0 my people, you 
shall know that l am the Lord. Then will I 
put Diy spirit into you, and you shall live; 
and i will settle you on your own land; and 
you shall know that I am the Lord. 1 have 
snoken it, and l will do it,’ is the oracle 
of the Lord." (37: 11 - 14) 

Despite the impatient clamour of the people, Yhwh has not 

forgotten them. The bones are dried up, not because the

the people have been abandoned by ihwh, but because they

But Yhwh will restore the people by reassembling the bones 
to the corporate unit (putting flesh on the skeletons,

of the exiles was "despair", and Yhwh’s answer was "rest¬ 
oration"®

In the x'romise of Kestoration (11: 13 - 21J Kzekiel 
cries out ^Aii Lord God, wilt thou make a complete end 
of the remnant of Israel?” (11: 13). As the text stands, 
this exclamation follows upon the death of Lelatiah after 
he had heard the prophecy againsttthe false counsellors 
of Israel (11: 1 - 12). The question arises as to who 
comprises the remant. Is the remnant referred to those 
who remained in Israel after the first deportation? 
after the second deportation? or is it the exiles in 
Babylon? For the purposes of this thesis it is not 
important that Ezekiel had in mind thosewho v/ere left 
in Jerusalem; what is important is whom Yhwh considered 
as the remnant, and close examination shov^ls that it was 
the exiles, not only in Babylon, but ^airiong the nations” 
(v,16) and ”among the lands where they have gone” (v.l6; 
see also v.l7). In vv. 16 - 21, ihwh replies to Ezekiel 
and the inhabitants of Jerusalem. The Remnant,the exiles, 
have been scattered far and wide by Ihwh, yet he will 
bring them back to the land of Israel. There they will 
cleanse the land and begin a., new life in proper relation¬ 
ship to Yhwh: ”then shall they be my people, and I will 
be their God,” (v. 20) But only those who are Virilling 
to go back in a spirit of obedience to Yhwh will live 
in the new land. ”And I will give them a new heart, and 
will put a nev/ spirit within them; 1 will remove the 
heart of stone from their flesh, so that they may follow

In the Allegory of the Two Sticks (37: 15 - 28) the 
restoration is also described. The children of Israel 
are to be gathered from the midst of the nations and 
brought back to their own land, which was given to Jacob 
(v.25). They will be a single nation, not two nations, 
and they will have a single king over them ”my servant 
David” (v.24). They shall no longer apostatize, but 
shall follow the ordinances and statutes of Yhv/h. This 
will be an everlasting kingdom and rule, with an ever- 
lasting covenant. Yhwh*s sanctuary will be in their

peopleV (vv. 26 - 27) ”And when my sanctuary is set in 
the midst of them forever, the nations shall know that I 
the Lord am setting Israel apart for myself.” (37: 28).

”When with the ever-circling years 
Comes round the Age of Gold.ty 

Although these lines are from a Christian hymn, (E. H. 
Sears’ It came upon the midnight clear ), they represent 
meither a Christian nor Old Testament view of history. 

The cyclic theory of history is largely Greek. Arist¬ 
otle thought of history as simply a record of events 
( de Poetica 1451). ”Consquently in Greek thought there 
is no viev^ of the world as history, even though there 
is no lack of historiography as a report of the confusion 
of human movements and as an example of politics. Even 
where the infinity of time threatens the picture of the 
circle, as illustrated in the idea of world-eras, the 
symbol of the circle is victorious in the idea of the 
’eternal recurrence of the same.’” (Paul Tillich, The

interpretation of history , p, 244). But in the Old 
Testament there is a concept of world-history, though 
it may not be clearly stated or developed. It is, in 
fact, a doctrine of history, the idea that history has 
a purpose in which" man is involved, and which he can 
to some extent know. Xhwh is the God of all the earth, 
and his r:)urpose is the purpose of history. This could 
be believed because Israel, from experience, knew Yhwh 
as a person (anthropomorphism being an attempt to ex¬ 
press personality), and only a person can have a purpose. 
And as a person he has direct relations with men^-he was 
the God of Abraham, Isaac, and Jacob. Of course it was 
recognized that there is a difference between the person¬ 
ality of fhwh and that of men, but it is not a sheer dif¬ 
ference which would result in complete transcendence. 

But what is Yhwh*s purpose? The Old Testament has 
no final answer to this question. Bor is it concerned 
with Y^orld history as such to any. great extent. *'*It is 
interested in the purpose of God for Israel, and it bel¬ 
ieves that nothing in the process of history can inter¬ 
fere v/ith that purpose.” (Rowley, The He-niscovery of 
the Old Testament , p. 74). And it is in the purpose of 
Yhwh for Israel that the Old Testament makes any approach 
to a viev7 of world history; apart from that purpose, the 
Israelites had no interest in the events-of-the-years in

There are two things which must be noticed about 

the purpose ol* ^hwh. rirst, man is the center of that 

nurpose, as is shown in J document, (especially the 

Creation Story), and i'salm 8. secondly Israel is the 

agent of the purpose: 

The Lord said to Abram: 

'^Leave your land, your relatives,and 
your father’s home, for the land, that i 
v/ill show you; and i v;ill malce a great 
nation of you; i will bless you, and 
make your name so great that it will be 
used for blessings, i will bless those 
who bless you, and anyone who curses 
you will 1 curse; through you shall all 
the families of the earth invoke bless¬ 
ings on one another.” (Genesis: 12: 1 - 3; 

J document). 

It is true that Israel did not always remember her agency, 

but it was never finally forgotten, for it was bound up 

with the concept of election, and in the doctrine of 

election Yhwh’s purpose is seen. 

A. jingle says: How odd of God to choose the Jewsl 

There is no ultimate answer to the question as to why 

Yhwh chose the Israelites, other than to say ^Because 

he is God.” This is expressed in various ways, all of 

which can be resolved to the self-consistency of ihwh. 

”It was not because you were the great¬ 
est of all peoples that the Lord set his heart 
on you and chose you (for you were the smallest 
of all peoples), but it v;as because the Lord 
loved you, and would keep the oath that he swore 
to your father...” (Deuteronomy 7: 7 - S) 

But this immediately raises the question ; why did Yhwh 

love Israel? A recurring answer is ”For his name’s sake.”

It is not possible to go beyond Yhwh and ask why he does 
things; he acts in accordance with his will, his plan, 
his purpose. The scandal of particularity can never be 
a live issue from the Biblical point of 'view; if Yhv/h was 
going to send Jesus Christ into the world, a particular 
time, place, and people had to be chosen and prepared. 

There is a question which it is legitimate to ask; 
for what purpose did Yhwh choose asrael? The Old Testa¬ 
ment has ananswer to this; Yhwh chose Israel that ne might 
reveal himself to her and through her to the rest of the 
world. This revelation was not static, but dynamic. It 
meant that Israel was to serve ihwh (Psalm 105} and to 
bring justice (true religion) to the Gentiles (Isaiah 42: 1). 
The service, then, has a missionary aspect; the chosen 
are to be a "light to lighten the Gentiles". By living 
in harmony with ihwh, the Israelites will develop a nation¬ 
al life that will attract other nations to the Light; 
it is not performed by the sending out of missionaries, 
which is a New Testament view. This is the extent of 
the Old Testament concept of world history; whatever 
events may have occurred in the past life of other nations 
at some time in the future they v/ould come to know and 
obey Yhwh through the mediation of Israel. 

The discussion in the earlier part of this chapter 
has shown Lzekiel’s conception of history, and only a few 
remarks are necessary here. In the Old Testament there

are two strands of tradition concerning the choosing of 
Israel. One looks to Abraham and faith, the other to 
moses and the covenant. Jilzekiel ignores the Abrahamic 
strain, and looks to the covenant: Yhwh chose Israel out 
of his love and entered into a covenant with her. But 
Israel did not keep the covenant. For this she is to be 
punished with a remedial type of punishment, and this will 
restore her to right relation with Yhwh. 

In trying to explain all this, to give the purpose 
for it, Ezekiel arrived at the formulae '’for my name’s 
sake”, ”for my honor”, ’’so shall you know that I am the 
Lord”. From a "practical” point of view, this meant that 
Israel was to be the means of all the nations knowing Yhwh 
to attempt to go beyond this results in coming back to 
the formulae noted. 

There is more than literary art in ^zekiel’s use of 
harlotry as a metaphor: he is not using the figure merely 
to attract attention. There is a definite reason why he 
chose this metaphor which had been so ably used by Hosea 
in his prophecies: it implies a definite relation of love, 
a relation which is basic to the theology of Ezekiel. For 
from it Israel knows Yhwh and his will. As in any true 
love relation, one partner does not act solely on the com¬ 
mands of the other: each acts upon a knowledge of the 
other partner, a knowledge which can come only from the 
love relation. Each intuits what the other wants and

requires to maintain the love. The love increases the 
knowledge, and the knov/ledge the love. To turn aside from 
this is harlotry, a debasing of love. The knowledge that 
Israel received was not doctrine: what was revealed to 
her was Yhwh himself. "So shall you knov/ that 1 am the 
Lord." 

This is a relationship between persons, in which 
each comes forth to meet the other, and in that meeting 
is true knowledge found. Aant grasped this idea to some 
extent when he concluded from his categorical imperative 
that people should not use other people as means, but as 
ends in themselves, however, this eventually falls short 
of the concept being discussed. To be sure, it is a 
common tendency to regard a person as a means, but when 
this occurs, the fuller meaning of personality (the know¬ 
ledge derived from the love relationship) is lost. For 
the Old Testament this tendency resulted in an attempt 
to categorize Yhwh, to say that he is the same as other 
gods, with their limitations and attributes, and to 
attempt to use Yhwh as an "ever-present help in time of 
trouble" who could be ignored at other times. TYiis ten¬ 
dency and attempt are due, according to Ezekiel (and most 
of the Old Testament) to pride: Israel wanted to maintain 
her relationship with ihwh, yet at the same time to go 
outside of it. But these are contradictory desires. The 
implication is that Israel thought she could know what

was good for herself apart from the knowledge of the 
Highest. Yhwh was not trying to use Israel in the ordin¬ 
ary sense of that word. From the love relationship, 

Israel had knowledge of Yhwh which was to lead ' her to do 
certain things for him (and he for her), resulting in a 
type of life which would bring the other nations into 
relationship with him. 

The analogy of the human love relation, even though 
it is of the ideal, itust not be pressed too far since Yhwh 
is not a human being. Vfnen anthropomorphism is used cor¬ 
rectly, it is always with the tacit assumption that Yhwh 
is more than the terms involve, a recognition that person¬ 
ality is the highest concept of being which is available, 
and that even this concept cannot contain fully the at¬ 
tributes of Yhwh. To insist that it can and does is to 
attempt wO limit and define the nature of the Deity, an 
attempt to make God in the image of man. In the ideal 
human love relation one partner cannot finally and com¬ 
pletely define the other, since such an endeavour strains 
at the meaning of words, and results in a weakening of 
the love relation. It implies: ^^Beyond this limit there 
can be no further knowledge of this person,^ Yet true 
lovers maintain that love increases over the years. ’^Love 
will never die out,” says Paul in his inspired Hymn of 
Love (1 Corinthians 13). 

This throws light on the meaning of the covenant.

As Rowley points out in The .biblical Doctrine of Election , 
ch, 2, the prophetic ideal covenant was not a contract 
or bargain of the sort that Jacob made with God at Bethel 
(Genesis 28: 20ff.). Rowley notes (loc.cit.) a statement 
from R.B.Yo Scott, The Relevance of the Prophets : ^*The 
covenant required a real sharing of life, and could not 
be thought of simply as a formal contract, conferring on 
Israel a special status and a claim upon her God,” 

Rather was it a testament or legacy which Yhwh drew up 
and offered to Israel, and Israel could accept or reject 
it. Her acceptance was to be manifested in her obedience 
to the terms of the legacy, the laws. Nor was this 
legacy received solely by one generation: it vms for all 
generations, and each generation was to renew it and keep 
it through obedience. This obedience was not to the 
laws themselves, but to the laws as part of the revelation 
of ihvm’s love. It was a response to IhwhVs love, a 
response in love to love. And it is in this sense that 
the decalogue (Exodus 20) must be understood. The first 
five commands deal with .the God-man relation, the next 
four with the resulting man-man relation, (the 'sanctity 
of life, marriage, property, and honor), while the last 
points out that envy is the characteristic which destroys 
the man-man relation. 


This explains Azekiel^s use of the term ^harlotry”

to describe Israel's breaking of the covenant. Ihe 
prophet might not have argued in the above abstract manner, 
but this is what he meant in essence. i!'or the iiebrev^ 
mind was neither speculative nor analytical in the mod¬ 
ern (Greek] sense; the Hebrew looked at life from within 
life, as a participant, not as a spectator, it is the 
lover, not the celibate, speaking of love. 

And it is in this context, this concept of Yhwh and 
his purpose and love, that Hzekiel sees the relation be¬ 
tween ^hwh and the nation of xsrael. The purpose of Yhwh 
is seen in history and is fulfilled through his love. 
Hzekiel turns to history (the covenant] to see the purpose, 
so that he can interpret current events (the exile) to 
the Israelites, and prophesy the restoration.

THE GENTILS NATIONS 








52 


CHilPTER V 

THE GENTILE NATIONS 


”You see, r/iacPhee, if one is thinking 
simply of goohness in the abstract, one soon 
reaches the fatal idea of something stand¬ 
ardized—some common kind of life to which 
all nations ougnt to progress. Of course 
there are universal rules to which all good¬ 
ness must conform. But that^s only the 
grammar of virtue.” 

—C. S. Lewis, That 
Hideous Strength , p. 461 


In the first chapter of this work the proposition 
concerning the judgment of all nations was discussed. 

It was shown that Ezekiel believed that there was a 
basis for such judgment, a standard of conduct up to 
which all nations were expected to measure, a standard 
which was a revelation of*Yhwh. And it was shown that 
Yhwh expected this standard to be recognized and accepted. 
There was the implication that the nations could live 
up this or reject it; if the latter, punishment would 
follow- In the discussion of Israel’s case the argument 
was set forth that judgment and punishment come, not 
because of harsh justice on the part of ihv/h, but because 
of his love for his creation, and in accord with his 
divine purpose. This present chapter is to deal with 
the relationship between ^hwh and the gentile nations 
in detail.

It is often a problem for Christians to determine 
the relation betv^^een their religion and other religions, 
their God and other Gods. It was also a problem for 
some of the children of Israel, although the Old Testa¬ 
ment (especially the prophets) .has a definite answer in 
the doctrine of election. For example, the little story 
of Jonah, fanciful though it may be, certainly proclaims 
that not Israel alone is to be saved, but that the gentile 
nations are to have the word of Yhwh preached to them as 
well. Dante recognized this vv^hen he wrote: 

’♦Or, living before Christendom, their knees 
Paid not aright those tributes that belong 
To God; and I myself am one of these.” 

Yet Dante consigns those who did not have the opportunity 
to choose Christ to the first circle of Hell, near the 
upper limit. Dorothy L. Sayers corrmients: "They could 
not, that is, choose Christ; they could, and did, choose 
human virtue, and for that they have their reward. (Pagans 
who choose evil by their own standards are judged by 
these standards—cf. Rom. S: S - 15--and are found lower 
down). Here again, the souls’have v\?hat they choee^; they 
enjoy that kind of after-life which they themselves im¬ 
agined for the virtuous dead; their failure lay in not 
1 

imagining better”. It is not right to read Christianity 


1. The quotation and the commentary are from Lliss Sayer’s 
'translation and edition of Dante’s The Divine Comedy , 
I: Hell, canto iv, lines 37-39. pp. 9S, 95. Virgil 
is speaking in the quotation.

back into the Old Testament, but on the other hand it 
is sheer folly to claim that the ultimate basis of 
judgment is different in the two Testaments. 

’^It is not for your sake that I am about 
to act, 0 house of Israel, but for my holy 
name which you have caused to be profaned 
among the nations to which you came. I will 
restore the holiness of my great name which 
has been profaned among the nations—^the 
name which you have caused to be profaned in 
the midst of them—and when 1 restore my 
holiness in their sight, through my dealings 
with you, the nations shall know that 1 am 
the Lord.’’ (56: 28 - 23) 

This is a statement of Yhwh^s Grand Plan, that not only 

Israel but the gentiles as well, shall know that he is 

the Lord. Yhwh will set his sanctuary in the midst of 

Israel, ’’And when my sanctuary is set in the midst of 

them forever, the nations shall know that I the^Lord>am 

setting Israel apart for myself”. (37: 28). But there 

Is more to the relation between Yhwh and the gentiles 

than just this. 

The proposition of judgment implied that it is pos¬ 
sible for any nation to sin against Yhwh, and Ezekiel 
states that this has come to pass. The nations have not 
lived up to the standard that he has set for them. This 
is seen in the statement about the sin of Israel: ”Yet 
she has wickedly rebelled against my ordinances and 
statutes more than the nations and lands that are round 
about her ....”(5: 6). ’’Because you have been more reb¬ 
ellious than the nations that are round about you....”

(5: 7)o Though Israel may have sinned more, the gentiles 
are also guilty of rebelling against ^hwh • How did they 
do this? Various ways are mentioned* 

There were social injustice and lewd practices, liv¬ 
ing ”in pride, plenty, and thoughtless ease; they sup¬ 
ported not the poor and needy; they grew haughty and com¬ 
mitted abomination before me.*,”.(16: 49). 

There was their attitude towards Jerusalem; "Because 
the enemy jeered over you, saying, ’Ahal the ancient 
heights have become our possession.’" They "with intense 
glee and maliciousness of heart took over my land as a 
possession for themselves, to hold it as a prey." "...I 
will no longer permit the taunts of the nations to be 
heard against you (Israel), and no longer shall you have 
to bear the reproach of the peoples..,". (36: 2,5,15). 

There was their attitude of general belligerence, 
aggressive warfare against one another and against Israel. 
They "stuck terror into the land of the living" (32: 23 - 
32). ‘There was their failure to support Israel in her 
time of need (29: 6 - 7). And there was their particip¬ 
ation as the "lovers" in the downfall of Jerusalem, giv¬ 
ing in to the solicitations of the Israelites (16: 15 - 43). 

But the accusations against the gentiles are not 
made in general statements alone; many nations and their 
sins are mentioned particularly, along with their punish¬ 
ments, and these will be examined as briefly as possible.

Sodom was guilty of social injustice, but slie is to 
be restored to her fortune (16: 44 - 54). No punishment 
is mentioned here, but it is likely that the ancient des¬ 
truction of the city described in Genesis 19 was in the 
mind of the prophet. 

Because Ammon laughed and jeered (said nvil , an 
onomatopoetic word of joy over the misfortunes of another, 
cf. Psalm 35: 21, 25) at the desolation of Ihv^h’s sanc¬ 
tuary, and at the desolation of -i-srael and Judah, she is 
to be handed over to the hedemites for punishment. This 
punishment is to be destructive and apparently complete— 
as they are being destroyed, they will know ^’that i am 
the Lord"\ But sucja knowledge will not be saving know¬ 
ledge of the kind Israel receives in her punishment. 
will destroy you utterly.” (25: 7). This doesn^t mean, 
necessarily, that every individual Ammonite will be an¬ 
nihilated, but likely that as a nation they will be des¬ 
troyed—those who are left alive will be assimilated in¬ 
to other peoples and so lose their national identity. 

(21: 28 - 32). 

Moab is dealt with in 25: 8 - 11. This nation said: 
"Behold, the house of Judah has become like other nations.” 
(25: 8). Though this was, in a sense, true, it was a 
failure to recognize Judah’s relation to Yhwh. Like Ammon, 
Moab is to be handed over to the Kedemites, and to be des¬

from Egypt. To what extent Ezekiel was mistaken is of 
little concern here; what is of concern is the interpret¬ 
ation (the mood or tone of the interpretation) that 
Ezekiel was putting on history. His prophecy wasn’t com¬ 
pletely wrong--Xviebudchadnezzar at least attacked Tyre, 
and the prophet, being human and hating Tyre, may have 
got a bit over-enthusiastic.

made by Ezekiel in some cases, here he tends to merge 
the two. The ruin of Egypt is described in 30: 1 - 26.

Assyria, Elam, Meschech and Tubal, who "struck terror 
into the land of the living". They are uncircumsized. 
There are Edom, all the princes of the north, and the 
Sidonians. Generalizing, it will be seen that all these 
nations were powerful, all were uncircumsized, and all 
had "struck terror into the land of the living." /uid 
"Pharaoh and "all his teeming multitude" (i.e., Egypt) 
are classified with these nations. The reasons for the 
punishment of Egypt are, then, that she failed to sup¬ 
port Israel in time of trouble, and "struck terror into 
the land of the living.” Therefore she is to be pun¬ 
ished and "shall know that I am the Lord." (30: 26). 

The Overthrov/ of Gog, (ch. 38) is one of the most 
descriptive and imaginative passages in the v^hole of 
Ezekiel’s work. It is not, strictly speaking, a prophecy, 
since it is not set in a historic context as is the 
case with the great prophecies, e.g. tferemiah knew that 
the Scythians were coming down the coast. But here, the 
prophet is casting far into the future, beyond such im¬ 
mediate knowledge. This is an example of the beginnings 
of apocalyptic, a form of writing in which the prophet 
tries to see the final victory of Good despite the seem¬ 
ingly ovei^whelming power of present Evil, belief that 
Yhwh’s purpose will triiiniphthough all current events 
point to the contrary. This type of thinking was stim¬ 
ulated by unfulfilled prophecy: the prophets believed

that all prophecies would be fulfilled, but as the pas¬ 
sing years did not bring the fulfilment, they pushed the 
events into the future. Ezekiel pictures the battle with 
G-og as occurring after the restoration, when Israel is 
”a land restored from desolation, and inhabited by a 
people gathered from many a nation...all of them living 
in security,” (38: 9). 

The word of Yhwh comes to Ezekiel, telling him to 
set his ”face toward Gog, of the land of Magog, the great 
prince of Meshech and Tubal, and prophesy against him, 
and say...’I am against you, 0 Gog, the great prince of 
Meschech and Tubal*”, (38: 2 - 3). Gog is to assemble 
his army, including the people of Persia, Cush, and Put, 
and to prepare for a mighty invasion. There is some dis¬ 
pute as to the identity of Gog and these other nations. 

In his notes on Ezekiel in the Century Bible , Lofthouse 
identifies Tubal with ”the Tibarenes, dwelling from the 
Black Sea to Cilicia”, and Meshech as a land east of 
Tubal; Corner is the Cimmerians (Armenians) as in Homer’s 
Odyssey xi. 14, and this is parallel with Togarmah; Cush 
is Ethiopia, and Put a Hamitic tribe. If the actual 
identity of these nations is not conclusively known, it 
is still obvious that the reference is t6 distant barb¬ 
arians. Because Ihwh is in control of history and all 
nations, this preparation and invasion is also his doing. 
This great horde is to come against the now peaceful

Israel in a mighty battle and be defeated. On the sur¬ 
face of the account, no reason is given for this terrible 
catastrophe in which the very elements are involved: 

will proceed against him also with 
pestilence and bloodshed; and 1 will rain upon 
him and his hordes, and upon the many peoples 
that are with him, a lashing rain, accompanied 
by hailstones, fire, and brimstone. So will 
I reveal my greatness and my holiness, and v/ill 
make myself known in the sight of many nations; 
and they shall know that I am the Lord.^’ 

(38: 22,23)o 

For it is to be a terrible slaughter: the weapons will 
supply the Israelites with fire-wood for seven years 
(39: 9 - 10) and it will take seven months to bury the 
bodies of the slain (39: 11 - 16), even though a valley 
(The Valley of Gog’s Mob) is used for a grave, and a 
standing coimnission is set up to hunt out the corpses. 

Yet no specific evil is recorded of these people, and to 
say the event is solely to enhance Yhwh’s honor is cont¬ 
rary to the thought of hzekiel. A closer examination, 
however, gives a clue to the meaning of the demolition. 
Standing on the ground of the Utopian society of the 
restored Israel, and looking at the world in the light 
of Yhwh’s holiness, it will be noticed that several things 
come into focus. The destruction of the invaders involves 
and results in the revealing of Yhwh’s holiness, his name, 
his glory (38: 16,23; 39: 7, 13, etc. ) • Nov; this holiness 
cannot abide that which is unclean, impure, evil, unholy, 
and since the holiness is contrasted with the invaders.

philosophers today. I^ior should it be thought that their 
writings and utterances, as preserved in the Old Testament, 
contain all their thoughts. This means that the relation

centuries later that another inspired man could briefly 
state the case: 

For God loved the world so much that he gave his 
only Son, so that no one who believes in him 
should be lost, but that they should all have 
eternal life. For God did not send his Son 
into the world to pass judgment upon the world, 
but that through him the world might be saved. 

(John 3: 16 -= 17).

CHAPTER VI 

GOD AND THE INDIVIDUAL 

It was by the river Chebar that Ezekiel received his 
call to be a prophet. (1: Iff). Lofthouse and Cooke, in 
their works already cited, point out that the exact loc¬ 
ation is not now known, nor has the river raentioned been 
finally identified. Both of these raen, however, think 
that the Chebar v;as a large canal or watercourse near 
Babylon, and that it was along the banks of this that the 
captives were set down. 

Ezekiel describes his call in the opening chapters 

of his book. It was a vision (1; 1), and he emphasizes 

this by the use of such words as ^’semblance^’ (1; 5,22,28), 

and ^^appearance^’ (1: 19,27). lhat he saw must not be 

taken as an objective reality in the sense that someone 

else present would have seen it. But that does not mean 

that it was not real, unless reality is restricted to that 

which can be experienced by the five senses, a concept 

with which the prophets would not agree. In Perelandra , 

C. S. Lewis describes the attempt of spiritual beings to 

appear to Ransom, the hero, on the planet Venus. 

A tornado of sheer monstrosities seemed to be 
pouring over Ransom. Darting pillars filled 
with eyes, lightning pulsations, talons and 
beaks and billowy masses of what suggested 
snow, volleyed through cubes and heptagons

into an infinite black void. "Stop it...stop 
it," he yelled, and the scene cleared. He 
gazed around blinking on the field of lilies, 
and presently gave the eldila to understand 
that this kind of appearance was not suited 
to huiiian sensations. "Look then on this," 
said the voices again. And he looked with 
some reluctance, and far off between the peaks 
on the other side of the little valley there 
came rolling wheels. There was nothing but 
that--concentric wheels moving with a rather 
sickening slowness one inside the other.... 

He bade them try yet a third tirae. And sud¬ 
denly two human figures stood before him on 
the opposite side of the lake. 

"But do I see you as you really are?" 
he asked. 

"Only maleldil sees any creature as it 
really is," said Mars. 

"How do you see one another?" asked 
Ransom. 

"There are no holding places in your mind 
for an answer to that." 

"Am 1 then seeing only an appearance? Is 
it not real at all?" 

"You see only an appearance, small one. 

You have never seen more than an appearance 
of anything--not of Arbol, nor of a stone, 
nor of your own body. This appearance is as 
true as what you see of those." 

(pp. 227, 232) 

It is important to notice that Ezekiel uses the verb 
ilMj for "I looked" "I saw" etc., and the noun 

T r T* f - 

derived from this verb for "vision". As with the Eng¬ 
lish verb to see , this verb is used in the Hebrew for 
the perception of physical objects as well as what is 
perceived in imagination. Ezekiel was not dreaming, for 
that would require the use of the verb ff , which 

“ T 

involves the idea of what is seen while asleep. The 
prophet does not use this latter verb because he is stres-

^^Such was the semblance of the glory of the Lord, as 
it aripeared to me.” (1: 28). It is not difficult to 
sketch this thing that the prophet saw—wheels, four¬ 
headed winged creatures, firmament, throne, etc. But 
having done so, it would still be necessary to ask what 
it’s significance was. The key to this lies in the use 
of the word ”glory”. Although, obviously, what Lzekiel 
saw was a throne-car with ”a semblance like that of a 
man sitting upon it”, (v.26), he does not refer to it as 
such, but always as the ”glory”. Therefore, this word 
must be Investigated. 

A great deal has been written on the meaning of 
”glory” in both the Old and i^ew Testaments. The Hebrew 
root is D be heavy,^ .weighty, burdensome, honoured , 
and is used extensively throughout the Old Testament. 

(In the New Testament and Septuagint, So^oc is used.) 

The problem lies in the use of the word in a wide variety 
of contexts. German scholars have tended to trace the 
meaning of the word through the evolutionary theory, with 
rather dubious results, since it involves the as yet 
unsolved problem of the dating of the documents. 

The root may be used of the weight of grief (lob 6: 3), 
or of Yhwh’s hand in punishment (1 Samuel 5: 11). It 
is used of the inconvenience that some people cause others

Now for the use of the word in reference to Ihwh. 
moses asked to see ihwh’s glory, and was told: 

will make all my goodliness pass before 
t^ view, and will proclaim the name of the 
Lord before you, and how i am gracious to those 
to whom 1 am gracious, and compassionate with 
those with whom i am compassionate...! will put 
you in a cleft of the rock, and cover you with 
my hand until 1 pass by; then 1 will take away 
my hand, so that you may see my back, while my 
face shall not be seen.^’ (Exodus 33: 18 - 23). 

This is rrom J Document. '?ihat this may have meant orig¬ 
inally, assuming an oral tradition prior to the v^riting 
of the document, is not possible to say. but from the 
point of view of the developed theology of the Old Testa 
ment it may be interpreted. Moses is to see the glory 
of Yhwh, but he must not expect to see his face. Yhwh 
passes by, i.e., he does something, is actively present 
in a situation, but it is not until he has ^^leff’ that 
this is recognized. This is evident from the parallel¬ 
ism of ideas in the passage quoted.

following results. In the morning, the people see the 
manna lying on the ground, and the cloud of mist slov/ly 
rising. Here is the activity of Yhwh: he has been pres¬ 
ent. The cloud is associated with the glory of Yhwh: 

^^The glory of the Lord appeared in the cloud. (v.lO). 
-Anyone who has seen a cloud of mist rising will perceive 
the idea of heaviness involved. In this passage, then, 
the glory contains the ideas of presence, activity, and 
weight, or summarily, authoritative influence. 

In the call of the prophet Isaiah {Isaiah 6: 1 - 13), 
the seraphim call to one another: 

Holy, holy, holy, is the Lord of hosts; 

The whole earth is full of his glory. 

(6:3). 

But this translation does violence to Hebrew grammar* 

It should read "The fulness of the whole earth is his 
glory" because of the use of the construct . This then 
becomes parallel to the opening lines of the nineteenth 
Fsalm: 

The heavens are telling the glory of Ood, 

And the sky shows forth the work of his hands. 

Day lonto day pours forth speech. 

And night unto night declares knowledge. 

The creative activity of Yhwh is his glory: y^hat he has 

done witnesses to what he is. It must not be thought 

that Yhwh has stopped his activity, of course, for the 

whole of the Old Testament is a witness to his continuing 

work in his world. And each new act declares his glory.

is what men see of him: it is the outward and visible 
sign of his inv/ard and invisible nature. 

It is evident that Ezekiel had these ideas in mind, 
for he says that he ’^saw visions of God’^ {1: 1), which 
he goes on to describe as the glory of Yhvvrh. It was a 
manifestation of the divinity, and, as such, it had a 
purpose--the revelation of the word of Yhwh to the pro¬ 
phet. It is now necessary to investigate the meaning of 
**the word of the Lord^^. 

To begin with, it is essential to understand some¬ 
thing of the meaning of inspiration. , R. E. M. Harding 
in An Anatomy of Inspiration , deals with the meaning of 
inspiration in the lives of great artists. As she points 
out, inspiration is not in itself the revelation, but 
the preparation for, or circumstances under which, the 
revelation comes. Her findings may be summarized. The 
ideas received come with suddenness, frequently in half- 
sleep or sleep. They come with a sense of compulsion 
and possession. Often there are.dramatic changes in out¬ 
ward appearance (as was the case with Beethoven, for 
example). Sometimes it may amount to vision or audition. 
And there is alvmys the sense of complete conviction. 

Now although Miss Harding is dealing with artists, it 
is remarkable how closely her analysis fits the knowledge 
of the inspiration of the great prophets as we have it.

The essential difference seems to lie in the content of 
the revelation. iVhereas the artists receive themes for 
great artistic works - symphonies, paintings, stories, 
poems,, etc.,—the prophets received that which is known 
as ”the v;ord of the Lord'*. 

The Hebrew word for "word" is 1 ^ J » from the 
verb 1 3. T speak s But the noun means more than some¬ 
thing spoken, it may mean something done, an act. Thus 
the title of the Book of Chronicles is, literally, "The 
Words (or Acts) of the Days". "Take care lest a base 
thought (lit. *a word, a useless thing*) enter your head 
(heart) like this...and you behave meanly to your needy 
country-man...." (Deuteronomy 15: 9). Here the meaning 
is obviously of an intended or planned action. "Isn’t 
this what we told you in Egypt would happen...?" (Exodus 
14: 12); literally: "Is not this the word we spoke to you 
in Egypt...?" This refers both to the objections of the 
children of Israel to coming out of Egypt, and to the pur¬ 
suit by Pharaoh, and hence involves both meanings of 
**word". 

Eor the Hebrew, a word was almost a tangible thing. 
"So Joab put the words into her mouth" (2 Samuel 14: 3). 
"Thus Samuel grew, and the Lord was with him, and he let 
none of his words fall to the ground." (1 Samuel 3: 19). 
This implies that if Yhwh was not with Samuel, the words 
would "fall to the ground." That is, the words of men
